<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the general language (macrolanguage). For specific varieties of Arabic and other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Arabic_(disambiguation)" title="Arabic (disambiguation)">Arabic (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

href="/wiki/Tajiki_Arabic" title="Tajiki Arabic">Tajiki Arabic</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" <p><b>Arabic</b> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">اَلْعَرَبِيَّةُ</span></span>, <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-ʿarabiyyah</i></span></i> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="ar-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[al ʕaraˈbijːa]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Al_arabic.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/Al_arabic.ogg" title="Al arabic.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">عَرَبِيّ</span></span>, <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ʿarabīy</i></span></i> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="ar-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ˈʕarabiː]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arabi.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/Arabi.ogg" title="Arabi.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span> or <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="ar-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ʕaraˈbij]</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic language</a> spoken primarily across the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stefan_Weninger_2011_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stefan_Weninger_2011-5">[5]</a></sup> Having emerged in the 1st century, it is named after the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arab people</a>; the term "Arab" was initially used to describe those living in the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, as perceived by geographers from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 7th century, Arabic has been characterized by <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a>, with an opposition between a standard <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">prestige language</a>—i.e. Literary Arabic: <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a> (MSA) or <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[a]</a></sup>—and diverse <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> varieties, which serve as <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">mother tongues</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVersteegh2014viii_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVersteegh2014viii-9">[8]</a></sup> Colloquial dialects vary significantly from MSA, impeding mutual intelligibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> MSA is only acquired through formal education and is not spoken natively. It is the language of literature, official documents, and formal written media. In spoken form, MSA is used in formal contexts, news bulletins and for prayers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> This variety is the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">liturgical language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> It is an official language of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_where_Arabic_is_an_official_language" title="List of countries where Arabic is an official language">26 states</a> and 1 disputed territory, <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_the_number_of_countries_in_which_they_are_recognized_as_an_official_language" title="List of languages by the number of countries in which they are recognized as an official language">the third most</a> after English and French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> It is also one of six <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_the_United_Nations" title="Official languages of the United Nations">official languages of the United Nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Spoken varieties are the usual medium of communication in all other domains. They are not standardized and vary significantly, some of them being <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually unintelligible</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">International Organization for Standardization</a> assigns language codes to 33 <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">varieties of Arabic</a>, including MSA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www-01.sil.org_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-www-01.sil.org-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Arabic vernaculars do not descend from MSA or Classical Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Combined, Arabic dialects have 362 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_speakers" title="Native speakers">native speakers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-e25_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-e25-1">[1]</a></sup> while MSA is spoken by 274 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L2_speakers" title="L2 speakers">L2 speakers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arb_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-arb-2">[2]</a></sup> making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_total_number_of_speakers" title="List of languages by total number of speakers">sixth most spoken language in the world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arabic is traditionally written with the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic alphabet</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right-to-left" title="Right-to-left">right-to-left</a> <a href="/wiki/Abjad" title="Abjad">abjad</a>. This alphabet is the official script for MSA. Colloquial varieties were traditionally not written, however, with the emergence of social media, the amount of written dialects has significantly increased online. Besides the Arabic alphabet, dialects are also often written in <a href="/wiki/Arabic_chat_alphabet" title="Arabic chat alphabet">Latin</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_to_right" title="Left to right">left to right</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew characters</a> (in Israel)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShachmonMack2016p223–224_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-ShachmonMack2016p223–224-3">[3]</a></sup> with no standardized orthography. <a href="/wiki/Maltese_language" title="Maltese language">Maltese</a> is the only colloquial variety officially written in <a href="/wiki/Maltese_alphabet" title="Maltese alphabet">a Latin alphabet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Čéplö_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Čéplö-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_Arabic_languages" title="Classification of Arabic languages">Classification of Arabic languages</a></div>
<p>Arabic is usually classified as a <a href="/wiki/Central_Semitic_languages" title="Central Semitic languages">Central Semitic language</a>. Linguists still differ as to the best classification of Semitic language sub-groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stefan_Weninger_2011_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stefan_Weninger_2011-5">[5]</a></sup> The Semitic languages changed significantly between <a href="/wiki/Proto-Semitic_language" title="Proto-Semitic language">Proto-Semitic</a> and the emergence of Central Semitic languages, particularly in grammar. Innovations of the Central Semitic languages—all maintained in Arabic—include:
</p>
<ol><li>The conversion of the suffix-conjugated stative formation (<i>jalas-</i>) into a past tense.</li>
<li>The conversion of the prefix-conjugated <a href="/wiki/Preterite" title="Preterite">preterite</a>-tense formation (<i>yajlis-</i>) into a present tense.</li>
<li>The elimination of other prefix-conjugated mood/aspect forms (e.g., a present tense formed by doubling the middle root, a <a href="/wiki/Perfect_(grammar)" title="Perfect (grammar)">perfect</a> formed by <a href="/wiki/Infix" title="Infix">infixing</a> a <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span> after the first root consonant, probably a jussive formed by a stress shift) in favor of new moods formed by endings attached to the prefix-conjugation forms (e.g., <i>-u</i> for indicative, <i>-a</i> for subjunctive, no ending for jussive, <i>-an</i> or <i>-anna</i> for energetic).</li>
<li>The development of an internal passive.</li></ol>
<p>There are several features which Classical Arabic, the modern Arabic varieties, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Safaitic" title="Safaitic">Safaitic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hismaic_(Old_Arabic)" title="Hismaic (Old Arabic)">Hismaic</a> inscriptions share which are unattested in any other Central Semitic language variety, including the <a href="/wiki/Dadanitic" title="Dadanitic">Dadanitic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taymanitic" title="Taymanitic">Taymanitic</a> languages of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijaz" title="Hijaz">northern Hejaz</a>. These features are evidence of common descent from a <a href="/wiki/Proto-language" title="Proto-language">hypothetical ancestor</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Arabic" title="Proto-Arabic">Proto-Arabic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAl-Jallad2020a8_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAl-Jallad2020a8-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> The following features can be reconstructed with confidence for Proto-Arabic:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>negative particles <i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">m</span></i> * <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/mā/</span>; <i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">lʾn</span></i> *<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/lā-ʾan/</span> to Classical Arabic <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">lan</span></li>
<li><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">mafʿūl</span> G-passive participle</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Preposition_and_postposition" title="Preposition and postposition">prepositions</a> and adverbs <i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">f</span></i>, <i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ʿn</span></i>, <i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ʿnd</span></i>, <i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ḥt</span></i>, <i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ʿkdy</span></i></li>
<li>a subjunctive in -<i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">a</span></i></li>
<li><i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">t</span></i>-demonstratives</li>
<li>leveling of the -<i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">at</span></i> <a href="/wiki/Allomorph" title="Allomorph">allomorph</a> of the feminine ending</li>
<li><i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ʾn</span></i> <a href="/wiki/Complementizer" title="Complementizer">complementizer</a> and subordinator</li>
<li>the use of <i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">f</span></i>- to introduce modal clauses</li>
<li>independent object pronoun in <i><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">(ʾ)y</span></i></li>
<li>vestiges of <i><a href="/wiki/Nunation" title="Nunation">nunation</a></i></li></ol>
<p>On the other hand, several Arabic varieties are closer to other Semitic languages and maintain features not found in Classical Arabic, indicating that these varieties cannot have developed from Classical Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBirnstiel2019368_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirnstiel2019368-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> Thus, Arabic <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernaculars</a> do not descend from Classical Arabic:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVersteegh2014172_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVersteegh2014172-29">[28]</a></sup> Classical Arabic is a sister language rather than their direct ancestor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAl-Jallad2020a8_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAl-Jallad2020a8-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Arabic">Old Arabic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Arabic" title="Old Arabic">Old Arabic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Safaitic_script_with_a_figure_of_a_camel_on_a_red_sandstone_fragment,_from_es-Safa,_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3692" data-file-width="5219" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Safaitic_script_with_a_figure_of_a_camel_on_a_red_sandstone_fragment%2C_from_es-Safa%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg/220px-Safaitic_script_with_a_figure_of_a_camel_on_a_red_sandstone_fragment%2C_from_es-Safa%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Safaitic_script_with_a_figure_of_a_camel_on_a_red_sandstone_fragment%2C_from_es-Safa%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg/330px-Safaitic_script_with_a_figure_of_a_camel_on_a_red_sandstone_fragment%2C_from_es-Safa%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Safaitic_script_with_a_figure_of_a_camel_on_a_red_sandstone_fragment%2C_from_es-Safa%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg/440px-Safaitic_script_with_a_figure_of_a_camel_on_a_red_sandstone_fragment%2C_from_es-Safa%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Safaitic_script_with_a_figure_of_a_camel_on_a_red_sandstone_fragment,_from_es-Safa,_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Safaitic inscription</div></div></div>
<p>Arabia boasted a wide variety of Semitic languages in antiquity. In the southwest, various <a href="/wiki/Central_Semitic_languages" title="Central Semitic languages">Central Semitic languages</a> both belonging to and outside of the <a href="/wiki/Old_South_Arabian" title="Old South Arabian">Ancient South Arabian</a> family (e.g. Southern Thamudic) were spoken. It is also believed that the ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Modern_South_Arabian_languages" title="Modern South Arabian languages">Modern South Arabian languages</a> (non-Central Semitic languages) were also spoken in southern Arabia at this time. To the north, in the oases of northern <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dadanitic" title="Dadanitic">Dadanitic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taymanitic" title="Taymanitic">Taymanitic</a> held some prestige as inscriptional languages. In <a href="/wiki/Najd" title="Najd">Najd</a> and parts of western Arabia, a language known to scholars as Thamudic C is attested. In eastern Arabia, inscriptions in a script derived from ASA attest to a language known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasaitic_dialect" title="Hasaitic dialect">Hasaitic</a>. Finally, on the northwestern frontier of Arabia, various languages known to scholars as Thamudic B, Thamudic D, <a href="/wiki/Safaitic" title="Safaitic">Safaitic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hismaic" title="Hismaic">Hismaic</a> are attested. The last two share important isoglosses with later forms of Arabic, leading scholars to theorize that Safaitic and Hismaic are in fact early forms of Arabic and that they should be considered <a href="/wiki/Old_Arabic" title="Old Arabic">Old Arabic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linguists generally believe that "Old Arabic" (a collection of related dialects that constitute the precursor of Arabic) first emerged around the 1st century CE. Previously, the earliest attestation of Old Arabic was thought to be a single 1st century CE inscription in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sabaic" title="Sabaic">Sabaic script</a> at <a href="/wiki/Qaryat_al-Faw" title="Qaryat al-Faw">Qaryat Al-Faw</a>, in southern present-day Saudi Arabia. However, this inscription does not participate in several of the key innovations of the Arabic language group, such as the conversion of Semitic mimation to nunation in the singular. It is best reassessed as a separate language on the Central Semitic dialect continuum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>
It was also thought that Old Arabic coexisted alongside—and then gradually displaced--<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigraphic" title="Epigraphic">epigraphic</a> <a href="/wiki/Ancient_North_Arabian" title="Ancient North Arabian">Ancient North Arabian</a> (ANA), which was theorized to have been the regional tongue for many centuries. ANA, despite its name, was considered a very distinct language, and mutually unintelligible, from "Arabic". Scholars named its variant dialects after the towns where the inscriptions were discovered (<a href="/wiki/Dadanitic" title="Dadanitic">Dadanitic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taymanitic" title="Taymanitic">Taymanitic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hismaic" title="Hismaic">Hismaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safaitic" title="Safaitic">Safaitic</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stefan_Weninger_2011_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stefan_Weninger_2011-5">[5]</a></sup> However, most arguments for a single ANA language or language family were based on the shape of the definite article, a prefixed h-. It has been argued that the h- is an archaism and not a shared innovation, and thus unsuitable for language classification, rendering the hypothesis of an ANA language family untenable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> Safaitic and Hismaic, previously considered ANA, should be considered <a href="/wiki/Old_Arabic" title="Old Arabic">Old Arabic</a> due to the fact that they participate in the innovations common to all forms of Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-30">[29]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Epitaph_Imru-l-Qays_Louvre_AO4083.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="3680" decoding="async" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Epitaph_Imru-l-Qays_Louvre_AO4083.jpg/220px-Epitaph_Imru-l-Qays_Louvre_AO4083.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Epitaph_Imru-l-Qays_Louvre_AO4083.jpg/330px-Epitaph_Imru-l-Qays_Louvre_AO4083.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Epitaph_Imru-l-Qays_Louvre_AO4083.jpg/440px-Epitaph_Imru-l-Qays_Louvre_AO4083.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Epitaph_Imru-l-Qays_Louvre_AO4083.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Namara_inscription" title="Namara inscription">Namara inscription</a>, a sample of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabataean_alphabet" title="Nabataean alphabet">Nabataean script</a>, considered a direct precursor of Arabic script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup></div></div></div><p>The earliest attestation of continuous Arabic text in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabataean_alphabet" title="Nabataean alphabet">ancestor of the modern Arabic script</a> are three lines of poetry by a man named Garm(')allāhe found in <a href="/wiki/Avdat" title="Avdat">En Avdat, Israel</a>, and dated to around 125 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> This is followed by the <a href="/wiki/Namara_inscription" title="Namara inscription">Namara inscription</a>, an epitaph of the <a href="/wiki/Lakhmids" title="Lakhmids">Lakhmid</a> king Imru' al-Qays bar 'Amro, dating to 328 CE, found at <a href="/wiki/Namara_inscription" title="Namara inscription">Namaraa</a>, Syria. From the 4th to the 6th centuries, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabataean_alphabet" title="Nabataean alphabet">Nabataean script</a> evolves into the Arabic script recognizable from the early Islamic era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> There are inscriptions in an undotted, 17-letter Arabic script dating to the 6th century CE, found at four locations in Syria (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zabad,_Syria&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zabad, Syria (page does not exist)">Zabad</a>, Jabal 'Usays, <a href="/wiki/Harran,_as-Suwayda" title="Harran, as-Suwayda">Harran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umm_el-Jimal" title="Umm el-Jimal">Umm al-Jimaal</a>). The oldest surviving papyrus in Arabic dates to 643 CE, and it uses dots to produce the modern 28-letter Arabic alphabet. The language of that papyrus and of the Qur'an are referred to by linguists as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quranic_Arabic" title="Quranic Arabic">Quranic Arabic</a>", as distinct from its codification soon thereafter into "<a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stefan_Weninger_2011_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stefan_Weninger_2011-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Hejazi_and_Classical_Arabic">Old Hejazi and Classical Arabic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Hijazi" title="Old Hijazi">Old Hijazi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg/220px-Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg/330px-Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg/440px-Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_Quran_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arabic from the Quran in the old Hijazi dialect (Hijazi script, 7th century AD)</div></div></div>
<p>In late pre-Islamic times, a transdialectal and transcommunal variety of Arabic emerged in the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>, which continued living its parallel life after literary Arabic had been institutionally standardized in the 2nd and 3rd century of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Hijra</a>, most strongly in Judeo-Christian texts, keeping alive ancient features eliminated from the "learned" tradition (Classical Arabic).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[36]</a></sup> <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">This variety and both its classicizing and "lay" iterations have been termed Middle Arabic in the past, but they are thought to continue an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Higazi" title="Old Higazi">Old Higazi</a> register. It is clear that the orthography of the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Qur'an</a> was not developed for the standardized form of Classical Arabic; rather, it shows the attempt on the part of writers to record an archaic form of Old Higazi.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Folio_Blue_Quran_Met_2004.88.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2581" data-file-width="3791" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Folio_Blue_Quran_Met_2004.88.jpg/220px-Folio_Blue_Quran_Met_2004.88.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Folio_Blue_Quran_Met_2004.88.jpg/330px-Folio_Blue_Quran_Met_2004.88.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Folio_Blue_Quran_Met_2004.88.jpg/440px-Folio_Blue_Quran_Met_2004.88.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Folio_Blue_Quran_Met_2004.88.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Qur'an has served and continues to serve as a fundamental reference for Arabic. (<a href="/wiki/Maghrebi_script" title="Maghrebi script">Maghrebi</a> <a href="/wiki/Kufic" title="Kufic">Kufic</a> script, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Qur%27an" title="Blue Qur'an">Blue Qur'an</a>, 9th-10th century)</div></div></div>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">In the late 6th century AD, a relatively uniform intertribal "poetic koine" distinct from the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">spoken vernaculars</a> developed based on the <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> dialects of <a href="/wiki/Najd" title="Najd">Najd</a>, probably in connection with the court of <a href="/wiki/Al-Hirah" title="Al-Hirah">al-Ḥīra</a>. During the first Islamic century, the majority of Arabic poets and Arabic-writing persons spoke Arabic as their mother tongue. Their texts, although mainly preserved in far later manuscripts, contain traces of non-standardized <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a> elements in morphology and syntax.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standardization">Standardization</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arabic_script_evolution.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="817" data-file-width="728" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Arabic_script_evolution.svg/250px-Arabic_script_evolution.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Arabic_script_evolution.svg/376px-Arabic_script_evolution.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Arabic_script_evolution.svg/501px-Arabic_script_evolution.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arabic_script_evolution.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Evolution of early <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a> (9th–11th century), with the <i><a href="/wiki/Basmala" title="Basmala">Basmala</a></i> as an example, from <a href="/wiki/Kufic" title="Kufic">kufic</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an"><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">Qur'ān</i></span></a></i> manuscripts:  (1) Early 9th century, script with no dots or diacritic marks;(2) and (3) 9th–10th century under Abbasid dynasty, <a href="/wiki/Abu_al-Aswad_al-Du%27ali" title="Abu al-Aswad al-Du'ali">Abu al-Aswad's</a> system established red dots with each arrangement or position indicating a different short vowel; later, a second black-dot system was used to differentiate between letters like <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">fā’</i></span></i> and <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">qāf</i></span></i>;  (4) 11th century, in <a href="/wiki/Al-Khalil_ibn_Ahmad_al-Farahidi" title="Al-Khalil ibn Ahmad al-Farahidi">al-Farāhidi's</a> system (system used today) dots were changed into shapes resembling the letters to transcribe the corresponding long vowels.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abu_al-Aswad_al-Du%27ali" title="Abu al-Aswad al-Du'ali">Abu al-Aswad al-Du'ali</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 603</span>–689) is credited with standardizing <a href="/wiki/Arabic_grammar" title="Arabic grammar">Arabic grammar</a>, or <i>an-naḥw</i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">النَّحو</span>‎</span></span> "the way"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup>), and pioneering a system of <a href="/wiki/Arabic_diacritics" title="Arabic diacritics">diacritics</a> to differentiate consonants (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">نقط الإعجام</span>‎</span></span> <i>nuqat l-i'jām</i> "pointing for non-Arabs") and indicate <a href="/wiki/Arabic_diacritics#Tashkil_(marks_used_as_phonetic_guides)" title="Arabic diacritics">vocalization</a> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">التشكيل</span>‎</span></span> <i>at-tashkil</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Khalil_ibn_Ahmad_al-Farahidi" title="Al-Khalil ibn Ahmad al-Farahidi">Al-Khalil ibn Ahmad al-Farahidi</a> (718 – 786) compiled the first Arabic dictionary, <a href="/wiki/Kitab_al-%27Ayn" title="Kitab al-'Ayn"><i>Kitāb al-'Ayn</i></a> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">كتاب العين</span>‎</span></span> "The Book of the Letter <a href="/wiki/Ayin" title="Ayin">ع</a>"), and is credited with establishing the rules of Arabic <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">prosody</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Jahiz" title="Al-Jahiz">Al-Jahiz</a> (776-868) proposed to <a href="/wiki/Al-Akhfash_al-Akbar" title="Al-Akhfash al-Akbar">Al-Akhfash al-Akbar</a> an overhaul of the grammar of Arabic, but it would not come to pass for two centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-41">[40]</a></sup> The standardization of Arabic reached completion around the end of the 8th century. The first comprehensive description of the <i>ʿarabiyya</i> "Arabic", <a href="/wiki/Sibawayh" title="Sibawayh">Sībawayhi's</a> <i>al</i>-<i>Kitāb</i>, is based first of all upon a corpus of poetic texts, in addition to Qur'an usage and Bedouin informants whom he considered to be reliable speakers of the <i>ʿarabiyya</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-referenceworks.brillonline.com_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-referenceworks.brillonline.com-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread">Spread</span></h3>
<p>Arabic spread with the spread of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">early Muslim conquests</a>, Arabic gained vocabulary from <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-33">[32]</a></sup> In the early <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid period</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Classical Greek</a> terms entered Arabic through translations carried out at <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad's</a> <a href="/wiki/House_of_Wisdom" title="House of Wisdom">House of Wisdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 8th century, knowledge of Classical Arabic had become an essential prerequisite for rising into the higher classes throughout the Islamic world, both for Muslims and non-Muslims. For example, <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Andalusi</a> Jewish philosopher, authored works in <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Arabic_dialects" title="Judeo-Arabic dialects">Judeo-Arabic</a>—Arabic written in <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_alphabet" title="Hebrew alphabet">Hebrew script</a>—including his famous <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guide_for_the_Perplexed" title="The Guide for the Perplexed">The Guide for the Perplexed</a></i> (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020748213">.mw-parser-output .script-hebrew,.mw-parser-output .script-Hebr{font-family:"SBL Hebrew","SBL BibLit","Taamey Ashkenaz","Taamey Frank CLM","Frank Ruehl CLM","Ezra SIL","Ezra SIL SR","Keter Aram Tsova","Taamey David CLM","Keter YG","Shofar","David CLM","Hadasim CLM","Simple CLM","Nachlieli",Cardo,Alef,"Noto Serif Hebrew","Noto Sans Hebrew","David Libre",David,"Times New Roman",Gisha,Arial,FreeSerif,FreeSans}</style><span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%;">דלאלת אלחאירין</span>‎, <span title="Arabic-language text"><span lang="ar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">دلالة الحائرين</span>‎</span></span> <i>Dalālat al-ḥāʾirīn</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_Jinni" title="Ibn Jinni">Ibn Jinni</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a>, a pioneer in <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a>, wrote prolifically in the 10th century on Arabic morphology and phonology in works such as <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kit%C4%81b_Al-Mun%E1%B9%A3if&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kitāb Al-Munṣif (page does not exist)">Kitāb Al-Munṣif</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kit%C4%81b_Al-Mu%E1%B8%A5tasab&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kitāb Al-Muḥtasab (page does not exist)">Kitāb Al-Muḥtasab</a>, and</i> <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kit%C4%81b_Al-Kha%E1%B9%A3%C4%81%CA%BEi%E1%B9%A3&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kitāb Al-Khaṣāʾiṣ (page does not exist)">Kitāb Al-Khaṣāʾiṣ</a></i><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://ar.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%AE%D8%B5%D8%A7%D8%A6%D8%B5_(%D9%83%D8%AA%D8%A7%D8%A8)" title="ar:الخصائص (كتاب)">ar</a>]</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_Mada%27" title="Ibn Mada'">Ibn Mada'</a> of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Cordoba</a> (1116–1196) realized the overhaul of Arabic grammar first proposed by <a href="/wiki/Al-Jahiz" title="Al-Jahiz">Al-Jahiz</a> 200 years prior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Maghrebi lexicographer <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Manzur" title="Ibn Manzur">Ibn Manzur</a> compiled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lis%C4%81n_al-%CA%BFArab" title="Lisān al-ʿArab">Lisān al-ʿArab</a> (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1094882035">.mw-parser-output .script-Cprt{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Segoe UI Historic","Noto Sans Cypriot",Code2001}.mw-parser-output .script-Hano{font-size:125%;font-family:"Noto Sans Hanunoo",FreeSerif,Quivira}.mw-parser-output .script-Latf,.mw-parser-output .script-de-Latf{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Breitkopf Fraktur",UnifrakturCook,UniFrakturMaguntia,MarsFraktur,"MarsFraktur OT",KochFraktur,"KochFraktur OT",OffenbacherSchwabOT,"LOB.AlteSchwabacher","LOV.AlteSchwabacher","LOB.AtlantisFraktur","LOV.AtlantisFraktur","LOB.BreitkopfFraktur","LOV.BreitkopfFraktur","LOB.FetteFraktur","LOV.FetteFraktur","LOB.Fraktur3","LOV.Fraktur3","LOB.RochFraktur","LOV.RochFraktur","LOB.PostFraktur","LOV.PostFraktur","LOB.RuelhscheFraktur","LOV.RuelhscheFraktur","LOB.RungholtFraktur","LOV.RungholtFraktur","LOB.TheuerbankFraktur","LOV.TheuerbankFraktur","LOB.VinetaFraktur","LOV.VinetaFraktur","LOB.WalbaumFraktur","LOV.WalbaumFraktur","LOB.WeberMainzerFraktur","LOV.WeberMainzerFraktur","LOB.WieynckFraktur","LOV.WieynckFraktur","LOB.ZentenarFraktur","LOV.ZentenarFraktur"}.mw-parser-output .script-en-Latf{font-size:1.25em;font-family:Cankama,"Old English Text MT","Textura Libera","Textura Libera Tenuis",London}.mw-parser-output .script-it-Latf{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Rotunda Pommerania",Rotunda,"Typographer Rotunda"}.mw-parser-output .script-Lina{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Noto Sans Linear A"}.mw-parser-output .script-Linb{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Noto Sans Linear B"}.mw-parser-output .script-Ugar{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Segoe UI Historic","Noto Sans Ugaritic",Aegean}.mw-parser-output .script-Xpeo{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Segoe UI Historic","Noto Sans Old Persian",Artaxerxes,Xerxes,Aegean}</style><span class="Unicode"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">لسان العرب</span>‎</span>, "Tongue of Arabs"), a major reference <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">dictionary</a> of Arabic, in 1290.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Arabic">Neo-Arabic</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charles_A._Ferguson" title="Charles A. Ferguson">Charles Ferguson</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koine</a> theory claims that the modern Arabic dialects collectively descend from a single military koine that sprang up during the Islamic conquests; this view has been challenged in recent times. Ahmad al-Jallad proposes that there were at least two considerably distinct types of Arabic on the eve of the conquests: Northern and Central (Al-Jallad 2009). The modern dialects emerged from a new contact situation produced following the conquests. Instead of the emergence of a single or multiple koines, the dialects contain several sedimentary layers of borrowed and areal features, which they absorbed at different points in their linguistic histories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-referenceworks.brillonline.com_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-referenceworks.brillonline.com-42">[41]</a></sup> According to Veersteegh and Bickerton, colloquial Arabic dialects arose from <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">pidginized</a> Arabic formed from contact between Arabs and conquered peoples. Pidginization and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Creolization" title="Creolization">creolization</a> among Arabs and <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">arabized</a> peoples could explain relative morphological and phonological simplicity of vernacular Arabic compared to Classical and MSA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVersteegh2014299_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVersteegh2014299-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In around the 11th and 12th centuries in <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">al-Andalus</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Zajal" title="Zajal">zajal</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Muwashshah" title="Muwashshah"><i>muwashah</i></a> poetry forms developed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andalusian_Arabic" title="Andalusian Arabic">dialectical Arabic of Cordoba</a> and the Maghreb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nahda">Nahda</span></h3>

1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/%D9%87%D9%8A%D8%A6%D8%A9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%85%D8%AC%D9%85%D8%B9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%84%D8%BA%D9%88%D9%8A_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%85%D9%84%D9%83%D9%8A_%28%D9%85%D8%AC%D9%85%D8%B9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%84%D8%BA%D8%A9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B9%D8%B1%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A9_%D8%A8%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%82%D8%A7%D9%87%D8%B1%D8%A9%29_%D9%8A%D9%88%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%AF%D8%B4%D9%8A%D9%86%D9%87.png/440px-%D9%87%D9%8A%D8%A6%D8%A9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%85%D8%AC%D9%85%D8%B9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%84%D8%BA%D9%88%D9%8A_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%85%D9%84%D9%83%D9%8A_%28%D9%85%D8%AC%D9%85%D8%B9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%84%D8%BA%D8%A9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B9%D8%B1%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A9_%D8%A8%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%82%D8%A7%D9%87%D8%B1%D8%A9%29_%D9%8A%D9%88%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%AF%D8%B4%D9%8A%D9%86%D9%87.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D9%87%D9%8A%D8%A6%D8%A9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%85%D8%AC%D9%85%D8%B9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%84%D8%BA%D9%88%D9%8A_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%85%D9%84%D9%83%D9%8A_(%D9%85%D8%AC%D9%85%D8%B9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%84%D8%BA%D8%A9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B9%D8%B1%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A9_%D8%A8%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%82%D8%A7%D9%87%D8%B1%D8%A9)_%D9%8A%D9%88%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%AF%D8%B4%D9%8A%D9%86%D9%87.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coverage in <a href="/wiki/Al-Ahram" title="Al-Ahram">Al-Ahram</a> in 1934 of the inauguration of the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_the_Arabic_Language_in_Cairo" title="Academy of the Arabic Language in Cairo">Academy of the Arabic Language in Cairo</a>, an organization of major importance to the modernization of Arabic.</div></div></div><p>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Nahda" title="Nahda">Nahda</a></i> was a cultural and especially literary renaissance of the 19th century in which writers sought "to fuse Arabic and European forms of expression."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-50">[49]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/James_L._Gelvin" title="James L. Gelvin">James L. Gelvin</a>, "<i>Nahda</i> writers attempted to simplify the Arabic language and script so that it might be accessible to a wider audience."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-50">[49]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nasser_and_Taha_Hussein,_Nov_19_1959.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="401" data-file-width="430" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Nasser_and_Taha_Hussein%2C_Nov_19_1959.JPG/220px-Nasser_and_Taha_Hussein%2C_Nov_19_1959.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Nasser_and_Taha_Hussein%2C_Nov_19_1959.JPG/330px-Nasser_and_Taha_Hussein%2C_Nov_19_1959.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d3/Nasser_and_Taha_Hussein%2C_Nov_19_1959.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nasser_and_Taha_Hussein,_Nov_19_1959.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Taha_Hussein" title="Taha Hussein">Taha Hussein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a> were both staunch defenders of Standard Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup></div></div></div><p>In the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">industrial revolution</a> and European <a href="/wiki/Hegemony" title="Hegemony">hegemony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a>, pioneering Arabic presses, such as the <a href="/wiki/Amiri_Press" title="Amiri Press">Amiri Press</a> established by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali</a> (1819), dramatically changed the diffusion and consumption of Arabic <a href="/wiki/Arabic_literature" title="Arabic literature">literature</a> and publications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rifa%27a_al-Tahtawi" title="Rifa'a al-Tahtawi">Rifa'a al-Tahtawi</a> proposed the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Madrasat_al-Alsun" title="Madrasat al-Alsun">Madrasat al-Alsun</a> in 1836 and led a translation campaign that highlighted the need for a lexical injection in Arabic, to suit concepts of the industrial and post-industrial age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:22-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> In response, a number of Arabic academies modeled after the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_fran%C3%A7aise" title="Académie française">Académie française</a></i> were established with the aim of developing standardized additions to the Arabic lexicon to suit these transformations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> first in <a href="/wiki/Arab_Academy_of_Damascus" title="Arab Academy of Damascus">Damascus</a> (1919), then in <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_the_Arabic_Language_in_Cairo" title="Academy of the Arabic Language in Cairo">Cairo</a> (1932), <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Iraqi Academy of Sciences">Baghdad</a> (1948), <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Studies_and_Research_on_Arabization" title="Institute for Studies and Research on Arabization">Rabat</a> (1960), <a href="/wiki/Jordan_Academy_of_Arabic" title="Jordan Academy of Arabic">Amman</a> (1977), <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Academy_of_the_Arabic_Language_in_Khartum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Academy of the Arabic Language in Khartum (page does not exist)">Khartum</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://ar.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D9%85%D8%AC%D9%85%D8%B9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D9%84%D8%BA%D8%A9_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B9%D8%B1%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%A9_%D8%A8%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%AE%D8%B1%D8%B7%D9%88%D9%85" title="ar:مجمع اللغة العربية بالخرطوم">ar</a>]</span> (1993), and <a href="/wiki/Tunisian_Academy_of_Sciences,_Letters,_and_Arts" title="Tunisian Academy of Sciences, Letters, and Arts">Tunis</a> (1993).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-57">[56]</a></sup> <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">They review language development, monitor new words and approve inclusion of new words into their published standard dictionaries. They also publish old and historical Arabic manuscripts.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1997, a bureau of Arabization standardization was added to the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League_Educational,_Cultural_and_Scientific_Organization" title="Arab League Educational, Cultural and Scientific Organization">Educational, Cultural, and Scientific Organization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-57">[56]</a></sup> These academies and organizations have worked toward the <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabization</a> of the sciences, <a href="/wiki/Neologism" title="Neologism">creating terms</a> in Arabic to describe new concepts, toward the standardization of these new terms throughout the Arabic-speaking world, and toward the development of Arabic as a <a href="/wiki/World_language" title="World language">world language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-57">[56]</a></sup> This gave rise to what Western scholars call Modern Standard Arabic. From the 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabization</a> became a postcolonial nationalist policy in countries such as Tunisia, Algeria, Morocco,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> and Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="24411" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Arabic_Swadesh_list_1-100.webm" height="124" id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Arabic_Swadesh_list_1-100.webm/220px--Arabic_Swadesh_list_1-100.webm.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="616" data-framerate="1000" data-height="480" data-shorttitle="WebM 480P" data-title="SD WebM (480P)" data-transcodekey="480p.webm" data-width="854" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5c/Arabic_Swadesh_list_1-100.webm/Arabic_Swadesh_list_1-100.webm.480p.webm" type='video/webm; <h2><span id="Classical.2C_Modern_Standard_and_spoken_Arabic"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical,_Modern_Standard_and_spoken_Arabic">Classical, Modern Standard and spoken Arabic</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">Varieties of Arabic</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Arabic_dictionaries" title="List of Arabic dictionaries">List of Arabic dictionaries</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg/170px-Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg/255px-Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg/340px-Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flag of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>, used in some cases for the Arabic language</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Hejaz_1917.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Flag_of_Hejaz_1917.svg/170px-Flag_of_Hejaz_1917.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Flag_of_Hejaz_1917.svg/255px-Flag_of_Hejaz_1917.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Flag_of_Hejaz_1917.svg/340px-Flag_of_Hejaz_1917.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Hejaz_1917.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flag used in some cases for the Arabic language (Flag of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hejaz" title="Kingdom of Hejaz">Kingdom of Hejaz</a> 1916–1925).The flag contains the four <a href="/wiki/Pan-Arab_colors" title="Pan-Arab colors">Pan-Arab colors</a>: <a href="/wiki/Black" title="Black">black</a>, <a href="/wiki/White" title="White">white</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green" title="Green">green</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red" title="Red">red</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><i>Arabic</i> usually refers to Standard Arabic, which Western linguists divide into <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a> and Modern Standard Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kamusella_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kamusella-60">[59]</a></sup> It could also refer to any of a variety of regional vernacular <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">Arabic dialects</a>, which are not necessarily mutually intelligible.
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Classical Arabic is the language found in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, used from the period of <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Pre-Islamic Arabia">Pre-Islamic Arabia</a> to that of the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>. Classical Arabic is prescriptive, according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syntactic" title="Syntactic">syntactic</a> and grammatical norms laid down by classical grammarians (such as <a href="/wiki/Sibawayh" title="Sibawayh">Sibawayh</a>) and the vocabulary defined in classical dictionaries (such as the <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Manzur#Lisān_al-ʿArab" title="Ibn Manzur"><i>Lisān al-ʻArab</i></a>).</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Modern Standard Arabic (MSA) largely follows the grammatical standards of Classical Arabic and uses much of the same vocabulary. However, it has discarded some grammatical constructions and vocabulary that no longer have any counterpart in the spoken varieties and has adopted certain new constructions and vocabulary from the spoken varieties. Much of the new vocabulary is used to denote concepts that have arisen in the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">industrial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Post-industrial_society" title="Post-industrial society">post-industrial era</a>, especially in modern times. Due to its grounding in Classical Arabic, Modern Standard Arabic is removed over a millennium from everyday speech, which is construed as a multitude of dialects of this language. These dialects and Modern Standard Arabic are described by some scholars as not mutually comprehensible. The former are usually acquired in families, while the latter is taught in formal education settings. However, there have been studies reporting some degree of comprehension of stories told in the standard variety among preschool-aged children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> The relation between Modern Standard Arabic and these dialects is sometimes compared to that of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a> vernaculars (which became <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>) in medieval and early modern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kamusella_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kamusella-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">MSA is the variety used in most current, printed Arabic publications, spoken by some of the Arabic media across North Africa and the Middle East, and understood by most educated Arabic speakers. "Literary Arabic" and "Standard Arabic" (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">فُصْحَى</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">fuṣḥá</i></span></i>) are less strictly defined terms that may refer to Modern Standard Arabic or Classical Arabic.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Some of the differences between Classical Arabic (CA) and Modern Standard Arabic (MSA) are as follows:</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Certain grammatical constructions of CA that have no counterpart in any modern vernacular dialect (e.g., the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energetic_mood" title="Energetic mood">energetic mood</a>) are almost never used in Modern Standard Arabic.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;"><a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">Case</a> distinctions are very rare in Arabic vernaculars. As a result, MSA is generally composed without case distinctions in mind, and the proper cases are added after the fact, when necessary. Because most case endings are noted using final short vowels, which are normally left unwritten in the Arabic script, it is unnecessary to determine the proper case of most words. The practical result of this is that MSA, like English and <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>, is written in a strongly determined word order and alternative orders that were used in CA for emphasis are rare. In addition, because of the lack of case marking in the spoken varieties, most speakers cannot consistently use the correct endings in extemporaneous speech. As a result, spoken MSA tends to drop or regularize the endings except when reading from a prepared text.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">The numeral system in CA is complex and heavily tied in with the case system. This system is never used in MSA, even in the most formal of circumstances; instead, a significantly simplified system is used, approximating the system of the conservative spoken varieties.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<p>MSA uses much Classical vocabulary (e.g., <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">dhahaba</i></span></i> 'to go') that is not present in the spoken varieties, but deletes Classical words that sound obsolete in MSA. In addition, MSA has borrowed or coined many terms for concepts that did not exist in Quranic times, and MSA continues to evolve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Some words have been borrowed from other languages—notice that transliteration mainly indicates spelling and not real pronunciation (e.g., <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">فِلْم</span></span> <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">film</i></span></i> 'film' or <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ديمقراطية</span></span> <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">dīmuqrāṭiyyah</i></span></i> 'democracy').</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">However, the current preference is to avoid direct borrowings, preferring to either use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loan_translation" title="Loan translation">loan translations</a> (e.g., <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">فرع</span></span> <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">farʻ</i></span></i> 'branch', also used for the branch of a company or organization; <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">جناح</span></span> <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">janāḥ</i></span></i> 'wing', is also used for the wing of an airplane, building, air force, etc.), or to coin new words using forms within existing <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">roots</a> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">استماتة</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">istimātah</i></span></i> '<a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a>', using the root <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">موت</span></span> <i>m/w/t</i> 'death' put into the <a href='/wiki/Arabic_verbs#Formation_of_derived_stems_("forms")' title="Arabic verbs">Xth form</a>, or <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">جامعة</span></span> <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">jāmiʻah</i></span></i> 'university', based on <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">جمع</span></span> <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">jamaʻa</i></span></i> 'to gather, unite'; <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">جمهورية</span></span> <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">jumhūriyyah</i></span></i> 'republic', based on <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">جمهور</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">jumhūr</i></span></i> 'multitude'). An earlier tendency was to redefine an older word although this has fallen into disuse (e.g., <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">هاتف</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">hātif</i></span></i> 'telephone' &lt; 'invisible caller (in Sufism)'; <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">جريدة</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">jarīdah</i></span></i> 'newspaper' &lt; 'palm-leaf stalk').</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><i>Colloquial</i> or <i>dialectal</i> Arabic refers to the many national or regional varieties which constitute the everyday spoken language. Colloquial Arabic has many regional variants; geographically distant varieties usually differ enough to be <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually unintelligible</a>, and some linguists consider them distinct languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> However, research indicates a high degree of mutual intelligibility between closely related Arabic variants for native speakers listening to words, sentences, and texts; and between more distantly related dialects in interactional situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The varieties are typically unwritten. They are often used in informal spoken media, such as <a href="/wiki/Soap_opera" title="Soap opera">soap operas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Talk_show" title="Talk show">talk shows</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-65">[64]</a></sup> as well as occasionally in certain forms of written media such as poetry and printed advertising.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hassaniya_Arabic" title="Hassaniya Arabic">Hassaniya Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maltese_language" title="Maltese language">Maltese</a> are only varieties of modern Arabic to have acquired official status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> The Senegalese government adopted the Latin script to write Hassaniya<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> Maltese is spoken in (predominantly <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a>) <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> and written with the <a href="/wiki/Maltese_alphabet" title="Maltese alphabet">Latin script</a>. Linguists agree that it is a variety of spoken Arabic, descended from <a href="/wiki/Siculo-Arabic" title="Siculo-Arabic">Siculo-Arabic</a>, though it has experienced extensive changes as a result of sustained and intensive contact with Italo-Romance varieties, and more recently also with English. Due to "a mix of social, cultural, historical, political, and indeed linguistic factors," many Maltese people today consider their language Semitic but not a type of Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Čéplö_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Čéplö-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Even during Muhammad's lifetime, there were dialects of spoken Arabic. Muhammad spoke in the dialect of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, in the western <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a>, and it was in this dialect that the Quran was written. However, the dialects of the eastern Arabian peninsula were considered the most prestigious at the time, so the language of the Quran was ultimately converted to follow the eastern <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a>. It is this phonology that underlies the modern pronunciation of Classical Arabic. The phonological differences between these two dialects account for some of the complexities of Arabic writing, most notably the writing of the <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a> or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamzah" title="Hamzah">hamzah</a></i> (which was preserved in the eastern dialects but lost in western speech) and the use of <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">alif maqṣūrah</i></span></i> (representing a sound preserved in the western dialects but merged with <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ā</i></span></i> in eastern speech).</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_and_usage">Status and usage</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diglossia">Diglossia</span></h3>
<p>The sociolinguistic situation of Arabic in modern times provides a prime example of the linguistic phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a>, which is the normal use of two separate varieties of the same language, usually in different social situations. <i>Tawleed</i> is the process of giving a new shade of meaning to an old classical word. For example, <i>al-hatif</i> lexicographically, means the one whose sound is heard but whose person remains unseen. Now the term <i>al-hatif</i> is used for a telephone. Therefore, the process of <i>tawleed</i> can express the needs of modern civilization in a manner that would appear to be originally Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> In the case of Arabic, educated Arabs of any nationality can be assumed to speak both their school-taught Standard Arabic as well as their native dialects, which depending on the region may be mutually unintelligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> Some of these dialects can be considered to constitute separate languages which may have "sub-dialects" of their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">When educated Arabs of different dialects engage in conversation (for example, a Moroccan speaking with a Lebanese), many speakers <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">code-switch</a> back and forth between the dialectal and standard varieties of the language, sometimes even within the same sentence. Arabic speakers often improve their familiarity with other dialects via music or film.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The issue of whether Arabic is one language or many languages is politically charged, in the same way it is for the <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindi_language" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urdu_language" title="Urdu language">Urdu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">Croatian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a> and English, etc. In contrast to speakers of Hindi and Urdu who claim they cannot understand each other even when they can, speakers of the varieties of Arabic will claim they can all understand each other even when they cannot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> While there is a minimum level of comprehension between all Arabic dialects, this level can increase or decrease based on geographic proximity: for example, Levantine and Gulf speakers understand each other much better than they do speakers from the Maghreb. The issue of diglossia between spoken and written language is a significant complicating factor: A single written form, significantly different from any of the spoken varieties learned natively, unites a number of sometimes divergent spoken forms. For political reasons, Arabs mostly assert that they all speak a single language, despite significant issues of mutual incomprehensibility among differing spoken versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>From a linguistic standpoint, it is often said that the various spoken varieties of Arabic differ among each other collectively about as much as the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">This is an apt comparison in a number of ways. The period of divergence from a single spoken form is similar—perhaps 1500 years for Arabic, 2000 years for the Romance languages. Also, while it is comprehensible to people from the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, a linguistically innovative variety such as <a href="/wiki/Moroccan_Arabic" title="Moroccan Arabic">Moroccan Arabic</a> is essentially incomprehensible to Arabs from the <a href="/wiki/Mashriq" title="Mashriq">Mashriq</a>, much as French is incomprehensible to Spanish or Italian speakers but relatively easily learned by them. This suggests that the spoken varieties may linguistically be considered separate languages.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Status_in_the_Arab_world_vis-.C3.A0-vis_other_languages"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_in_the_Arab_world_vis-à-vis_other_languages">Status in the Arab world vis-à-vis other languages</span></h3>
