<p>The phoneme <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d͡ʒ/</span> is represented by the Arabic letter <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">jīm</i></span></i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D8%AC" title="ج">ج</a></span></span>) and has many standard pronunciations. <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiced postalveolar affricate">d͡ʒ</a>]</span> is characteristic of north Algeria, Iraq, and most of the Arabian peninsula but with an allophonic <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">ʒ</a>]</span> in some positions; <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">ʒ</a>]</span> occurs in most of the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and most of North Africa; and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">ɡ</a>]</span> is standard in Egypt, coastal Yemen, and western Oman. Generally this corresponds with the pronunciation in the colloquial dialects. In Sudan and Yemen, as well as in some Sudanese and Yemeni varieties, it may be either <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɡʲ]</span> or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_plosive" title="Voiced palatal plosive">ɟ</a>]</span>, representing the original pronunciation of Classical Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_2002_16_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_2002_16-107">[106]</a></sup> <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Foreign words containing <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">ɡ</a>/</span> may be transcribed with <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D8%AC" title="ج">ج</a></span></span>, <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D8%BA" title="غ">غ</a></span></span>, <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D9%83" title="ك">ك</a></span></span>, <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D9%82" title="ق">ق</a></span></span>, <span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%DA%AF" title="گ">گ</a></span></span>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%DD%A3" title="ݣ">ݣ</a></span>‎ or <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097707674" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">ڨ</span>‎, depending on the regional practice. In northern Egypt, where the Arabic letter <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">jīm</i></span></i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D8%AC" title="ج">ج</a></span></span>) is normally pronounced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">ɡ</a>]</span>, a separate phoneme <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">ʒ</a>/</span>, which may be transcribed with <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">چ</span></span>, occurs in a small number of mostly non-Arabic loanwords, e.g., <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʒakitta/</span> 'jacket'.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;"><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/θ/</span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ث</span></span>) can be pronounced as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">s</a>]</span>. In some places of Maghreb it can be also pronounced as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolar affricate">t͡s</a>]</span>.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/x/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɣ/</span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">خ,‎ غ</span></span>) are velar, post-velar, or uvular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_2002_18_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_2002_18-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">In many varieties, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ħ, ʕ/</span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ح,‎ ع</span></span>) are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epiglottal_consonant" title="Epiglottal consonant">epiglottal</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʜ, ʢ]</span> in Western Asia.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> is pronounced as velarized <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Velarized_alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Velarized alveolar lateral approximant">ɫ</a>]</span> in الله <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʔallaːh/</span>, the name of God, q.e. Allah, when the word follows <i>a</i>, <i>ā</i>, <i>u</i> or <i>ū</i> (after <i>i</i> or <i>ī</i> it is unvelarized: <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">بسم الله</span></span> <i>bismi l–lāh</i> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/bismillaːh/</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_2002_16_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_2002_16-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emphatic consonant <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/dˤ/</span> was actually pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɮˤ]</span>, or possibly <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[d͡ɮˤ]</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup>—either way, a highly unusual sound. The medieval Arabs actually termed their language <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">lughat al-ḍād</i></span></i> 'the language of the <a href="/wiki/%E1%B8%8C%C4%81d" title="Ḍād">Ḍād</a>' (the name of the letter used for this sound), since they thought the sound was unique to their language. (In fact, it also exists in a few other minority Semitic languages, e.g., Mehri.)
</p><p>Arabic has consonants traditionally termed "emphatic" <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tˤ, dˤ, sˤ, ðˤ/</span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ط,‎ ض,‎ ص,‎ ظ</span></span>), which exhibit simultaneous <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">pharyngealization</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[tˤ, dˤ, sˤ, ðˤ]</span> as well as varying degrees of <a href="/wiki/Velarization" title="Velarization">velarization</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[tˠ, dˠ, sˠ, ðˠ]</span> (depending on the region), so they may be written with the "Velarized or pharyngealized" diacritic (<big><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"> ̴</span></big>) as: <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t̴, d̴, s̴, ð̴/</span>. This simultaneous articulation is described as "Retracted Tongue Root" by phonologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> In some transcription systems, emphasis is shown by capitalizing the letter, for example, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/dˤ/</span> is written ⟨D⟩; in others the letter is underlined or has a dot below it, for example, ⟨<span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ḍ</i></span>⟩.
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Vowels and consonants can be phonologically short or long. Long (<a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">geminate</a>) consonants are normally written doubled in Latin transcription (i.e. bb, dd, etc.), reflecting the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_diacritics" title="Arabic diacritics">Arabic diacritic</a> mark <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">shaddah</i></span></i>, which indicates doubled consonants. In actual pronunciation, doubled consonants are held twice as long as short consonants. This consonant lengthening is phonemically contrastive: <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">قبل</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">qabila</i></span></i> 'he accepted' vs. <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">قبّل</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">qabbala</i></span></i> 'he kissed'.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Syllable_structure">Syllable structure</span></h4>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Arabic has two kinds of syllables: open syllables (CV) and (CVV)—and closed syllables (CVC), (CVVC) and (CVCC). The syllable types with two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morae" title="Morae">morae</a> (units of time), i.e. CVC and CVV, are termed <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_syllable" title="Heavy syllable">heavy syllables</a></i>, while those with three morae, i.e. CVVC and CVCC, are <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superheavy_syllable" title="Superheavy syllable">superheavy syllables</a></i>. Superheavy syllables in Classical Arabic occur in only two places: at the end of the sentence (due to <a href="/wiki/Pausa" title="Pausa">pausal</a> pronunciation) and in words such as <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">حارّ</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ḥārr</i></span></i> 'hot', <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">مادّة</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">māddah</i></span></i> 'stuff, substance', <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">تحاجوا</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">taḥājjū</i></span></i> 'they disputed with each other', where a long <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ā</i></span></i> occurs before two identical consonants (a former short vowel between the consonants has been lost). (In less formal pronunciations of Modern Standard Arabic, superheavy syllables are common at the end of words or before <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">clitic</a> suffixes such as <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">-nā</i></span></i> 'us, our', due to the deletion of final short vowels.)</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In surface pronunciation, every vowel must be preceded by a consonant (which may include the <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʔ]</span>). There are no cases of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hiatus_(linguistics)" title="Hiatus (linguistics)">hiatus</a> within a word (where two vowels occur next to each other, without an intervening consonant). Some words do have an underlying vowel at the beginning, such as the definite article <i>al-</i> or words such as <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">اشترا</span></span> <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ishtarā</i></span></i> 'he bought', <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">اجتماع</span></span> <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ijtimāʻ</i></span></i> 'meeting'. When actually pronounced, one of three things happens:
</p>
<ul><li>If the word occurs after another word ending in a consonant, there is a smooth transition from final consonant to initial vowel, e.g., <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">الاجتماع</span></span> <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-ijtimāʻ</i></span></i> 'meeting' <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/alid͡ʒtimaːʕ/</span>.</li>
<li>If the word occurs after another word ending in a vowel, the initial vowel of the word is <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">elided</a>, e.g., <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">بيت المدير</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">baytu (a)l-mudīr</i></span></i> 'house of the director' <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/bajtulmudiːr/</span>.</li>
<li>If the word occurs at the beginning of an utterance, a glottal stop <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʔ]</span> is added onto the beginning, e.g., <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">البيت هو</span></span> <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">al-baytu huwa ...</i></span></i> 'The house is ...' <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʔalbajtuhuwa ... /</span>.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stress">Stress</span></h4>
<p>Word stress is not phonemically contrastive in Standard Arabic. It bears a strong relationship to vowel length. The basic rules for Modern Standard Arabic are:
</p>
<ul><li>A final vowel, long or short, may not be stressed.</li>
<li>Only one of the last three syllables may be stressed.</li>
<li>Given this restriction, the last <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_syllable" title="Heavy syllable">heavy syllable</a> (containing a long vowel or ending in a consonant) is stressed, if it is not the final syllable.</li>
<li>If the final syllable is super heavy and closed (of the form CVVC or CVCC) it receives stress.</li>
<li>If no syllable is heavy or super heavy, the first possible syllable (i.e. third from end) is stressed.</li>
<li>As a special exception, in Form VII and VIII verb forms stress may not be on the first syllable, despite the above rules: Hence <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">in<u>ka</u>tab(a)</i></span></i> 'he subscribed' (whether or not the final short vowel is pronounced), <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">yan<u>ka</u>tib(u)</i></span></i> 'he subscribes' (whether or not the final short vowel is pronounced), <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">yan<u>ka</u>tib</i></span></i> 'he should subscribe (juss.)'. Likewise Form VIII <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ish<u>ta</u>rā</i></span></i> 'he bought', <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">yash<u>ta</u>rī</i></span></i> 'he buys'.</li></ul>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">These rules may result in differently stressed syllables when final case endings are pronounced, vs. the normal situation where they are not pronounced, as in the above example of <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">mak-<u>ta</u>-ba-tun</i></span></i> 'library' in full pronunciation, but <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><u>mak</u>-ta-ba(-tun)</i></span></i> 'library' in short pronunciation.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">The restriction on final long vowels does not apply to the spoken dialects, where original final long vowels have been shortened and secondary final long vowels have arisen from loss of original final <i>-hu/hi</i>.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Some dialects have different stress rules. In the Cairo (Egyptian Arabic) dialect a heavy syllable may not carry stress more than two syllables from the end of a word, hence <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">mad-<u>ra</u>-sah</i></span></i> 'school', <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">qā-<u>hi</u>-rah</i></span></i> 'Cairo'. This also affects the way that Modern Standard Arabic is pronounced in Egypt. In the Arabic of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sana%27a" title="Sana'a">Sanaa</a>, stress is often retracted: <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><u>bay</u>-tayn</i></span></i> 'two houses', <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><u>mā</u>-sat-hum</i></span></i> 'their table', <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ma-<u>kā</u>-tīb</i></span></i> 'desks', <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn"><u>zā</u>-rat-ḥīn</i></span></i> 'sometimes', <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">mad-<u>ra</u>-sat-hum</i></span></i> 'their school'. (In this dialect, only syllables with long vowels or diphthongs are considered heavy; in a two-syllable word, the final syllable can be stressed only if the preceding syllable is light; and in longer words, the final syllable cannot be stressed.)</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Levels_of_pronunciation">Levels of pronunciation</span></h4>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">The final short vowels (e.g., the case endings <i>-a -i -u</i> and mood endings <i>-u -a</i>) are often not pronounced in this language, despite forming part of the formal paradigm of nouns and verbs. The following levels of pronunciation exist:</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Full_pronunciation_with_pausa">Full pronunciation with pausa</span></h5>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">This is the most formal level actually used in speech. All endings are pronounced as written, except at the end of an utterance, where the following changes occur:</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Final short vowels are not pronounced. (But possibly an exception is made for feminine plural <i>-na</i> and shortened vowels in the jussive/imperative of defective verbs, e.g., <i>irmi!</i> 'throw!'".)</li>
<li>The entire indefinite noun endings <i>-in</i> and <i>-un</i> (with <a href="/wiki/Nunation" title="Nunation">nunation</a>) are left off. The ending <i>-an</i> is left off of nouns preceded by a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T%C4%81%CA%BE_marb%C5%AB%E1%B9%ADah" title="Tāʾ marbūṭah">tāʾ marbūṭah</a></i> ة (i.e. the <i>-t</i> in the ending <i>-at-</i> that typically marks feminine nouns), but pronounced as <i>-ā</i> in other nouns (hence its writing in this fashion in the Arabic script).</li>
<li>The <i>tāʼ marbūṭah</i> itself (typically of feminine nouns) is pronounced as <i>h</i>. (At least, this is the case in extremely formal pronunciation, e.g., some Quranic recitations. In practice, this <i>h</i> is usually omitted.)</li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_short_pronunciation">Formal short pronunciation</span></h5>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">This is a formal level of pronunciation sometimes seen. It is somewhat like pronouncing all words as if they were in pausal position (with influence from the <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">colloquial varieties</a>). The following changes occur:</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Most final short vowels are not pronounced. However, the following short vowels <i>are</i> pronounced:
<ul><li>feminine plural <i>-na</i></li>
<li>shortened vowels in the jussive/imperative of defective verbs, e.g., <i>irmi!</i> 'throw!'</li>
<li>second-person singular feminine past-tense <i>-ti</i> and likewise <i>anti</i> 'you (fem. sg.)'</li>
<li>sometimes, first-person singular past-tense <i>-tu</i></li>
<li>sometimes, second-person masculine past-tense <i>-ta</i> and likewise <i>anta</i> 'you (masc. sg.)'</li>
<li>final <i>-a</i> in certain short words, e.g., <i>laysa</i> 'is not', <i>sawfa</i> (future-tense marker)</li></ul></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Nunation" title="Nunation">nunation</a> endings <i>-an -in -un</i> are not pronounced. However, they <i>are</i> pronounced in adverbial accusative formations, e.g., <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">taqrīban</i></span></i> تَقْرِيبًا 'almost, approximately', <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ʻādatan</i></span></i> عَادَةً 'usually'.</li>
<li>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T%C4%81%CA%BE_marb%C5%AB%E1%B9%ADah" title="Tāʾ marbūṭah">tāʾ marbūṭah</a></i> ending ة is unpronounced, <i>except</i> in <a href="/wiki/Construct_state" title="Construct state">construct state</a> nouns, where it sounds as <i>t</i> (and in adverbial accusative constructions, e.g., <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ʻādatan</i></span></i> عَادَةً 'usually', where the entire <i>-tan</i> is pronounced).</li>
<li>The masculine singular <a href="/wiki/Arabic_grammar#Nisba" title="Arabic grammar">nisbah</a> ending <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">-iyy</i></span></i> is actually pronounced <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">-ī</i></span></i> and is unstressed (but plural and feminine singular forms, i.e. when followed by a suffix, still sound as <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">-iyy-</i></span></i>).</li>
<li><i>Full endings</i> (including case endings) occur when a <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">clitic</a> object or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Possessive_suffix" title="Possessive suffix">possessive suffix</a> is added (e.g., <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">-nā</i></span></i> 'us/our').</li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Informal_short_pronunciation">Informal short pronunciation</span></h5>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">This is the pronunciation used by speakers of Modern Standard Arabic in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extemporaneous" title="Extemporaneous">extemporaneous</a> speech, i.e. when producing new sentences rather than simply reading a prepared text. It is similar to formal short pronunciation except that the rules for dropping final vowels apply <i>even</i> when a <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">clitic</a> suffix is added. Basically, short-vowel case and mood endings are never pronounced and certain other changes occur that echo the corresponding colloquial pronunciations. Specifically:</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>All the rules for formal short pronunciation apply, except as follows.</li>
<li>The past tense singular endings written formally as <i>-tu -ta -ti</i> are pronounced <i>-t -t -ti</i>. But masculine <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ʾanta</i></span></i> is pronounced in full.</li>
<li>Unlike in formal short pronunciation, the rules for dropping or modifying final endings are also applied when a <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">clitic</a> object or possessive suffix is added (e.g., <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">-nā</i></span></i> 'us/our'). If this produces a sequence of three consonants, then one of the following happens, depending on the speaker's native colloquial variety:
<ul><li>A short vowel (e.g., <i>-i-</i> or <i>-ǝ-</i>) is consistently added, either between the second and third or the first and second consonants.</li>
<li>Or, a short vowel is added only if an otherwise unpronounceable sequence occurs, typically due to a violation of the <a href="/wiki/Sonority_hierarchy" title="Sonority hierarchy">sonority hierarchy</a> (e.g., <i>-rtn-</i> is pronounced as a three-consonant cluster, but <i>-trn-</i> needs to be broken up).</li>
<li>Or, a short vowel is never added, but consonants like <i>r l m n</i> occurring between two other consonants will be pronounced as a <a href="/wiki/Syllabic_consonant" title="Syllabic consonant">syllabic consonant</a> (as in the English words "butter bottle bottom button").</li>
<li>When a doubled consonant occurs before another consonant (or finally), it is often shortened to a single consonant rather than a vowel added. (However, Moroccan Arabic never shortens doubled consonants or inserts short vowels to break up clusters, instead tolerating arbitrary-length series of arbitrary consonants and hence Moroccan Arabic speakers are likely to follow the same rules in their pronunciation of Modern Standard Arabic.)</li></ul></li>
<li>The clitic suffixes themselves tend also to be changed, in a way that avoids many possible occurrences of three-consonant clusters. In particular, <i>-ka -ki -hu</i> generally sound as <i>-ak -ik -uh</i>.</li>
<li>Final long vowels are often shortened, merging with any short vowels that remain.</li>
<li>Depending on the level of formality, the speaker's education level, etc., various grammatical changes may occur in ways that echo the colloquial variants:
<ul><li>Any remaining case endings (e.g. masculine plural nominative <i>-ūn</i> vs. oblique <i>-īn</i>) will be leveled, with the oblique form used everywhere. (However, in words like <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ab</i></span></i> 'father' and <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">akh</i></span></i> 'brother' with special long-vowel case endings in the <a href="/wiki/Construct_state" title="Construct state">construct state</a>, the nominative is used everywhere, hence <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">abū</i></span></i> 'father of', <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">akhū</i></span></i> 'brother of'.)</li>
<li>Feminine plural endings in verbs and clitic suffixes will often drop out, with the masculine plural endings used instead. If the speaker's native variety has feminine plural endings, they may be preserved, but will often be modified in the direction of the forms used in the speaker's native variety, e.g. <i>-an</i> instead of <i>-na</i>.</li>
<li>Dual endings will often drop out except on nouns and then used only for emphasis (similar to their use in the colloquial varieties); elsewhere, the plural endings are used (or feminine singular, if appropriate).</li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colloquial_varieties">Colloquial varieties</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">Varieties of Arabic</a></div>

</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">As mentioned above, many spoken dialects have a process of <i>emphasis spreading</i>, where the "emphasis" (<a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">pharyngealization</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Emphatic_consonant" title="Emphatic consonant">emphatic consonants</a> spreads forward and back through adjacent syllables, pharyngealizing all nearby consonants and triggering the back allophone <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ(ː)</a>]</span> in all nearby <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_vowel" title="Low vowel">low vowels</a>. The extent of emphasis spreading varies. For example, in Moroccan Arabic, it spreads as far as the first full vowel (i.e. sound derived from a long vowel or diphthong) on either side; in many Levantine dialects, it spreads indefinitely, but is blocked by any <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_approximant" title="Voiced palatal approximant">j</a>/</span> or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless postalveolar fricative">ʃ</a>/</span>; while in Egyptian Arabic, it usually spreads throughout the entire word, including prefixes and suffixes. In Moroccan Arabic, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i u/</span> also have emphatic allophones <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[e~ɛ]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[o~ɔ]</span>, respectively.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Unstressed short vowels, especially <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i u/</span>, are deleted in many contexts. Many sporadic examples of short vowel change have occurred (especially <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span>→<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span> and interchange <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span>↔<span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>). Most Levantine dialects merge short /i u/ into <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ə/</span> in most contexts (all except directly before a single final consonant). In Moroccan Arabic, on the other hand, short <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span> triggers <a href="/wiki/Labialization" title="Labialization">labialization</a> of nearby consonants (especially <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">velar consonants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uvular_consonant" title="Uvular consonant">uvular consonants</a>), and then short /a i u/ all merge into <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ə/</span>, which is deleted in many contexts. (The labialization plus <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ə/</span> is sometimes interpreted as an underlying phoneme <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ŭ/</span>.) This essentially causes the wholesale loss of the short-long vowel distinction, with the original long vowels <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aː iː uː/</span> remaining as half-long <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aˑ iˑ uˑ]</span>, phonemically <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a i u/</span>, which are used to represent <i>both</i> short and long vowels in borrowings from Literary Arabic.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Most spoken dialects have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophthongized" title="Monophthongized">monophthongized</a> original <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aj aw/</span> to <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/eː oː/</span> in most circumstances, including adjacent to emphatic consonants, while keeping them as the original diphthongs in others e.g. <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">مَوْعِد</span></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/m<b>aw</b>ʕid/</span>. In most of the <a href="/wiki/Moroccan_Arabic" title="Moroccan Arabic">Moroccan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algerian_Arabic" title="Algerian Arabic">Algerian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tunisian_Arabic" title="Tunisian Arabic">Tunisian</a> (except <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> and Southeastern) Arabic dialects, they have subsequently merged into original <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/iː uː/</span>.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants_2">Consonants</span></h4>
<p>In most dialects, there may be more or fewer phonemes than those listed in the chart above. For example, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">g</a>]</span> is considered a native phoneme in most Arabic dialects except in Levantine dialects like Syrian or Lebanese where <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ج</span></span> is pronounced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">ʒ</a>]</span> and <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ق</span></span> is pronounced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">ʔ</a>]</span>. <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiced postalveolar affricate">d͡ʒ</a>]</span> or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">ʒ</a>]</span> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ج</span></span>) is considered a native phoneme in most dialects except in Egyptian and a number of Yemeni and Omani dialects where <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ج</span></span> is pronounced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">g</a>]</span>. <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[zˤ]</span> or <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ðˤ]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[dˤ]</span> are distinguished in the dialects of Egypt, Sudan, the Levant and the Hejaz, but they have merged as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ðˤ]</span> in most dialects of the Arabian Peninsula, Iraq and Tunisia and have merged as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[dˤ]</span> in Morocco and Algeria. The usage of non-native <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_bilabial_plosive" title="Voiceless bilabial plosive">p</a>]</span> <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">پ</span></span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_labiodental_fricative" title="Voiced labiodental fricative">v</a>]</span> <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ڤ</span></span> depends on the usage of each speaker but they might be more prevalent in some dialects than others. The Iraqi and Gulf Arabic also has the sound <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless postalveolar affricate">t͡ʃ</a>]</span> and writes it and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɡ]</span> with the Persian letters <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%DA%86" title="چ">چ</a></span></span> and <span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%DA%AF" title="گ">گ</a></span></span>, as in <span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">گوجة</span></span> <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">gawjah</i></span> "plum"; <span title="Persian-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="fa">چمة</span></span> <span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">chimah</i></span> "truffle".
</p><p>Early in the expansion of Arabic, the separate emphatic phonemes <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɮˤ]</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ðˤ]</span> coalesced into a single phoneme <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ðˤ]</span>. Many dialects (such as Egyptian, Levantine, and much of the Maghreb) subsequently lost <a href="/wiki/Interdental_consonant" title="Interdental consonant">interdental</a> <a href="/wiki/Fricative" title="Fricative">fricatives</a>, converting <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[θ ð ðˤ]</span> into <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[t d dˤ]</span>. Most dialects borrow "learned" words from the Standard language using the same pronunciation as for inherited words, but some dialects without interdental fricatives (particularly in Egypt and the Levant) render original <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[θ ð ðˤ dˤ]</span> in borrowed words as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[s z zˤ dˤ]</span>.
</p><p>Another key distinguishing mark of Arabic dialects is how they render the original velar and uvular plosives <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_plosive" title="Voiceless uvular plosive">q</a>/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiced postalveolar affricate">d͡ʒ</a>/</span> (Proto-Semitic <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">ɡ</a>/</span>), and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_plosive" title="Voiceless velar plosive">k</a>/</span>:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ق</span></span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_plosive" title="Voiceless uvular plosive">q</a>/</span> retains its original pronunciation in widely scattered regions such as Yemen, Morocco, and urban areas of the Maghreb. It is pronounced as a <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">ʔ</a>]</span> in several <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">prestige dialects</a>, such as those spoken in Cairo, Beirut and Damascus. But it is rendered as a voiced velar plosive <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">ɡ</a>]</span> in Persian Gulf, Upper Egypt, parts of the Maghreb, and less urban parts of the Levant (e.g. Jordan). In Iraqi Arabic it sometimes retains its original pronunciation and is sometimes rendered as a voiced velar plosive, depending on the word. Some traditionally Christian villages in rural areas of the Levant render the sound as <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_plosive" title="Voiceless velar plosive">k</a>]</span>, as do Shiʻi Bahrainis. In some Gulf dialects, it is palatalized to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiced postalveolar affricate">d͡ʒ</a>]</span> or <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">ʒ</a>]</span>. It is pronounced as a voiced uvular constrictive <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_uvular_fricative" title="Voiced uvular fricative">ʁ</a>]</span> in Sudanese Arabic. Many dialects with a modified pronunciation for <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_plosive" title="Voiceless uvular plosive">q</a>/</span> maintain the <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_plosive" title="Voiceless uvular plosive">q</a>]</span> pronunciation in certain words (often with religious or educational overtones) borrowed from the Classical language.</li>
<li><span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ج</span></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d͡ʒ/</span> is pronounced as an affricate in Iraq and much of the Arabian Peninsula but is pronounced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_plosive" title="Voiced velar plosive">ɡ</a>]</span> in most of North Egypt and parts of Yemen and Oman, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_postalveolar_fricative" title="Voiced postalveolar fricative">ʒ</a>]</span> in Morocco, Tunisia, and the Levant, and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_approximant" title="Voiced palatal approximant">j</a>]</span>, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[i̠]</span> in most words in much of the Persian Gulf.</li>
<li><span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ك</span></span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_velar_plosive" title="Voiceless velar plosive">k</a>/</span> usually retains its original pronunciation but is palatalized to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_postalveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless postalveolar affricate">t͡ʃ</a>/</span> in many words in Israel and the Palestinian Territories, Iraq, and countries in the eastern part of the Arabian Peninsula. Often a distinction is made between the suffixes <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/-ak/</span> ('you', masc.) and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/-ik/</span> ('you', fem.), which become <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/-ak/</span> and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/-it͡ʃ/</span>, respectively. In Sana'a, Omani, and Bahrani <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/-ik/</span> is pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/-iʃ/</span>.</li></ul>
<p>Pharyngealization of the emphatic consonants tends to weaken in many of the spoken varieties, and to spread from emphatic consonants to nearby sounds. In addition, the "emphatic" allophone <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ</a>]</span> automatically triggers pharyngealization of adjacent sounds in many dialects. As a result, it may difficult or impossible to determine whether a given <a href="/wiki/Coronal_consonant" title="Coronal consonant">coronal consonant</a> is phonemically emphatic or not, especially in dialects with long-distance emphasis spreading. (A notable exception is the sounds <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_plosive" title="Voiceless alveolar plosive">t</a>/</span> vs. <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">tˤ</a>/</span> in Moroccan Arabic, because the former is pronounced as an <a href="/wiki/Affricate" title="Affricate">affricate</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolar affricate">t͡s</a>]</span> but the latter is not.)
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fa33aalah_EN.pdf"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1754" data-file-width="1239" decoding="async" height="467" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Fa33aalah_EN.pdf/page1-330px-Fa33aalah_EN.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Fa33aalah_EN.pdf/page1-495px-Fa33aalah_EN.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Fa33aalah_EN.pdf/page1-660px-Fa33aalah_EN.pdf.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fa33aalah_EN.pdf" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Examples of how the Arabic root and form system works</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arabic_grammar" title="Arabic grammar">Arabic grammar</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_Arabic_2">Literary Arabic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a></div>
<p>As in other Semitic languages, Arabic has a complex and unusual <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> (i.e. method of constructing words from a basic <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">root</a>). Arabic has a <a href="/wiki/Nonconcatenative_morphology" title="Nonconcatenative morphology">nonconcatenative</a> "root-and-pattern" morphology: A root consists of a set of bare consonants (usually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triliteral" title="Triliteral">three</a>), which are fitted into a discontinuous pattern to form words. For example, the word for 'I wrote' is constructed by combining the root <b><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">k-t-b</i></span></b> 'write' with the pattern <b><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">-a-a-tu</i></span></b> 'I Xed' to form <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">katabtu</i></span></i> 'I wrote'. Other verbs meaning 'I Xed' will typically have the same pattern but with different consonants, e.g. <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">qaraʼtu</i></span></i> 'I read', <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">akaltu</i></span></i> 'I ate', <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">dhahabtu</i></span></i> 'I went', although other patterns are possible (e.g. <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">sharibtu</i></span></i> 'I drank', <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">qultu</i></span></i> 'I said', <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">takallamtu</i></span></i> 'I spoke', where the subpattern used to signal the past tense may change but the suffix <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">-tu</i></span></i> is always used).
</p><p>From a single root <b><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">k-t-b</i></span></b>, numerous words can be formed by applying different patterns:
</p>
<ul><li>كَتَبْتُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">katabtu</i></span></i> 'I wrote'</li>
<li>كَتَّبْتُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">kattabtu</i></span></i> 'I had (something) written'</li>
<li>كَاتَبْتُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">kātabtu</i></span></i> 'I corresponded (with someone)'</li>
<li>أَكْتَبْتُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">'aktabtu</i></span></i> 'I dictated'</li>
<li>اِكْتَتَبْتُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">iktatabtu</i></span></i> 'I subscribed'</li>
<li>تَكَاتَبْنَا <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">takātabnā</i></span></i> 'we corresponded with each other'</li>
<li>أَكْتُبُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">'aktubu</i></span></i> 'I write'</li>
<li>أُكَتِّبُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">'ukattibu</i></span></i> 'I have (something) written'</li>
<li>أُكَاتِبُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">'ukātibu</i></span></i> 'I correspond (with someone)'</li>
<li>أُكْتِبُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">'uktibu</i></span></i> 'I dictate'</li>
<li>أَكْتَتِبُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">'aktatibu</i></span></i> 'I subscribe'</li>
<li>نَتَكَتِبُ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">natakātabu</i></span></i> 'we correspond each other'</li>
<li>كُتِبَ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">kutiba</i></span></i> 'it was written'</li>
<li>أُكْتِبَ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">'uktiba</i></span></i> 'it was dictated'</li>
<li>مَكْتُوبٌ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">maktūbun</i></span></i> 'written'</li>
<li>مُكْتَبٌ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">muktabun</i></span></i> 'dictated'</li>
<li>كِتَابٌ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">kitābun</i></span></i> 'book'</li>
<li>كُتُبٌ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">kutubun</i></span></i> 'books'</li>
<li>كَاتِبٌ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">kātibun</i></span></i> 'writer'</li>
<li>كُتَّابٌ <i><span title="American Library Association – Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">kuttābun</i></span></i> 'writers'</li>
<li>مَكْتَبٌ <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">maktabun</i></span></i> 'desk, office'</li>
<li>مَكْتَبَةٌ <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">maktabatun</i></span></i> 'library, bookshop'</li>
<li>etc.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns_and_adjectives">Nouns and adjectives</span></h4>
<p>Nouns in Literary Arabic have three grammatical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noun_case" title="Noun case">cases</a> (<a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> [also used when the noun is governed by a preposition]); three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">numbers</a> (singular, dual and plural); two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gender_(grammar)" title="Gender (grammar)">genders</a> (masculine and feminine); and three "states" (indefinite, definite, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Status_constructus" title="Status constructus">construct</a>). The cases of singular nouns (other than those that end in long ā) are indicated by <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffixed</a> short vowels (/-u/ for nominative, /-a/ for accusative, /-i/ for genitive).
</p><p>The feminine singular is often marked by ـَة /-at/, which is pronounced as /-ah/ before a pause. Plural is indicated either through endings (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_plural" title="Sound plural">sound plural</a>) or internal modification (the <a href="/wiki/Broken_plural" title="Broken plural">broken plural</a>). Definite nouns include all proper nouns, all nouns in "construct state" and all nouns which are <a href="/wiki/Prefix" title="Prefix">prefixed</a> by the definite article اَلْـ /al-/. Indefinite singular nouns (other than those that end in long ā) add a final /-n/ to the case-marking vowels, giving /-un/, /-an/ or /-in/ (which is also referred to as <a href="/wiki/Nunation" title="Nunation">nunation</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanw%C4%ABn" title="Tanwīn">tanwīn</a>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">Adjectives</a> in Literary Arabic are marked for case, number, gender and state, as for nouns. However, the plural of all non-human nouns is always combined with a singular feminine adjective, which takes the ـَة /-at/  suffix.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">Pronouns</a> in Literary Arabic are marked for person, number and gender. There are two varieties, independent pronouns and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enclitic#Enclitic" title="Enclitic">enclitics</a>. Enclitic pronouns are attached to the end of a verb, noun or preposition and indicate verbal and prepositional objects or possession of nouns. The first-person singular pronoun has a different enclitic form used for verbs (ـنِي /-nī/) and for nouns or prepositions (ـِي /-ī/ after consonants, ـيَ /-ya/ after vowels).
</p><p>Nouns, verbs, pronouns and adjectives agree with each other in all respects. However, non-human plural nouns are grammatically considered to be feminine singular. Furthermore, a verb in a verb-initial sentence is marked as singular regardless of its semantic number when the subject of the verb is explicitly mentioned as a noun. Numerals between three and ten show "chiasmic" agreement, in that grammatically masculine numerals have feminine marking and vice versa.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">Verbs</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Arabic_verbs" title="Arabic verbs">Arabic verbs</a></div>
<p>Verbs in Literary Arabic are marked for person (first, second, or third), gender, and number. They are <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">conjugated</a> in two major paradigms (<a href="/wiki/Past" title="Past">past</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-past" title="Non-past">non-past</a>); two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_voice" title="Grammatical voice">voices</a> (active and passive); and six <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">moods</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indicative" title="Indicative">indicative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">imperative</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subjunctive" title="Subjunctive">subjunctive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irrealis_mood#Jussive" title="Irrealis mood">jussive</a>, shorter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energetic_mood" title="Energetic mood">energetic</a> and longer energetic), the fifth and sixth moods, the energetics, exist only in Classical Arabic but not in MSA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> There are also two <a href="/wiki/Participle" title="Participle">participles</a> (active and passive) and a <a href="/wiki/Verbal_noun" title="Verbal noun">verbal noun</a>, but no <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">infinitive</a>.
</p><p>The past and non-past paradigms are sometimes also termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfective" title="Perfective">perfective</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperfective" title="Imperfective">imperfective</a>, indicating the fact that they actually represent a combination of <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tense</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspect</a>. The moods other than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indicative" title="Indicative">indicative</a> occur only in the non-past, and the <a href="/wiki/Future_tense" title="Future tense">future tense</a> is signaled by prefixing سَـ <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">sa-</i></span></i> or سَوْفَ <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">sawfa</i></span></i> onto the non-past. The past and non-past differ in the form of the stem (e.g., past كَتَبـ<i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">katab-</i></span></i> vs. non-past ـكْتُبـ <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">-ktub-</i></span></i>), and also use completely different sets of affixes for indicating person, number and gender: In the past, the person, number and gender are fused into a single <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffixal</a> morpheme, while in the non-past, a combination of <a href="/wiki/Prefix" title="Prefix">prefixes</a> (primarily encoding person) and suffixes (primarily encoding gender and number) are used. The passive voice uses the same person/number/gender affixes but changes the vowels of the stem.
</p><p>The following shows a paradigm of a regular Arabic verb, كَتَبَ <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">kataba</i></span></i> 'to write'. In Modern Standard, the energetic mood (in either long or short form, which have the same meaning) is almost never used.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Derivation">Derivation</span></h4>
<p>Like other <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic languages</a>, and unlike most other languages, Arabic makes much more use of <a href="/wiki/Nonconcatenative_morphology" title="Nonconcatenative morphology">nonconcatenative morphology</a> (applying many templates applied roots) to <a href="/wiki/Morphological_derivation" title="Morphological derivation">derive</a> words than adding prefixes or suffixes to words.
</p><p>For verbs, a given root can occur in many different <a href="/wiki/Derived_stem" title="Derived stem">derived verb stems</a> (of which there are about fifteen), each with one or more characteristic meanings and each with its own templates for the past and non-past stems, active and passive participles, and verbal noun. These are referred to by Western scholars as "Form I", "Form II", and so on through "Form XV" (although Forms XI to XV are rare). These stems encode grammatical functions such as the <a href="/wiki/Causative" title="Causative">causative</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intensive" title="Intensive">intensive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reflexive_verb" title="Reflexive verb">reflexive</a>. Stems sharing the same root consonants represent separate verbs, albeit often semantically related, and each is the basis for its own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verb_conjugation" title="Verb conjugation">conjugational</a> paradigm. As a result, these derived stems are part of the system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derivational_morphology" title="Derivational morphology">derivational morphology</a>, not part of the <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflectional</a> system.
</p><p>Examples of the different verbs formed from the root كتب <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">k-t-b</i></span></i> 'write' (using حمر <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ḥ-m-r</i></span></i> 'red' for Form IX, which is limited to colors and physical defects):
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Most of these forms are exclusively <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a>
</caption>

<td>'he asks (someone) to write'
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Form II is sometimes used to create transitive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denominative_verb" title="Denominative verb">denominative verbs</a> (verbs built from nouns); Form V is the equivalent used for intransitive denominatives.
</p><p>The associated participles and verbal nouns of a verb are the primary means of forming new lexical nouns in Arabic. This is similar to the process by which, for example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_gerund" title="English gerund">English gerund</a> "meeting" (similar to a verbal noun) has turned into a noun referring to a particular type of social, often work-related event where people gather together to have a "discussion" (another lexicalized verbal noun). Another fairly common means of forming nouns is through one of a limited number of patterns that can be applied directly to roots, such as the "nouns of location" in <i>ma-</i> (e.g. <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">maktab</i></span></i> 'desk, office' &lt; <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">k-t-b</i></span></i> 'write', <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">maṭbakh</i></span></i> 'kitchen' &lt; <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ṭ-b-kh</i></span></i> 'cook').
</p><p>The only three genuine suffixes are as follows:
</p>
<ul><li>The feminine suffix <i>-ah</i>; variously derives terms for women from related terms for men, or more generally terms along the same lines as the corresponding masculine, e.g. <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">maktabah</i></span></i> 'library' (also a writing-related place, but different from <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">maktab</i></span></i>, as above).</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Arabic_grammar#Nisba" title="Arabic grammar">nisbah</a> suffix <i>-iyy-</i>. This suffix is extremely productive, and forms adjectives meaning "related to X". It corresponds to English adjectives in <i>-ic, -al, -an, -y, -ist</i>, etc.</li>
<li>The feminine <a href="/wiki/Arabic_grammar#Nisba" title="Arabic grammar">nisbah</a> suffix <i>-iyyah</i>. This is formed by adding the feminine suffix <i>-ah</i> onto nisba adjectives to form abstract nouns. For example, from the basic root <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">sh-r-k</i></span></i> 'share' can be derived the Form VIII verb <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ishtaraka</i></span></i> 'to cooperate, participate', and in turn its verbal noun <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ishtirāk</i></span></i> 'cooperation, participation' can be formed. This in turn can be made into a nisbah adjective <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ishtirākī</i></span></i> 'socialist', from which an abstract noun <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">ishtirākiyyah</i></span></i> 'socialism' can be derived. Other recent formations are <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">jumhūriyyah</i></span></i> 'republic' (lit. "public-ness", &lt; <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">jumhūr</i></span></i> 'multitude, general public'), and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaddafi" title="Gaddafi">Gaddafi</a>-specific variation <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">jamāhīriyyah</i></span></i> 'people's republic' (lit. "masses-ness", &lt; <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">jamāhīr</i></span></i> 'the masses', pl. of <i><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">jumhūr</i></span></i>, as above).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colloquial_varieties_2">Colloquial varieties</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">Varieties of Arabic</a></div>
<p>The spoken dialects have lost the case distinctions and make only limited use of the dual (it occurs only on nouns and its use is no longer required in all circumstances). They have lost the mood distinctions other than imperative, but many have since gained new moods through the use of prefixes (most often /bi-/ for indicative vs. unmarked subjunctive). They have also mostly lost the indefinite "nunation" and the internal passive.
</p><p>The following is an example of a regular verb paradigm in Egyptian Arabic.
